Too complex. Let's set MAX_OC_PER_THREAD = 128. Then max C_out = 16 * 128 = 2048. That's safe for most models. Register usage: 128 floats = 512 bytes. Other registers: dw_vals (16 floats), temporaries. Total maybe < 1KB per thread. 256 threads * 1KB = 256KB per block. Register limit per block is 64KB (65536 32-bit registers) = 256KB. Wait, 65536 registers per block? On sm_80, registers per SM = 65536 * 32-bit? Actually, each SM has 65,536 32-bit registers. A block can use up to 65,536 registers (on some archs, the limit per block is 64K). So 256 threads * 128 registers = 32,768 registers. That's within the limit. Plus other registers, maybe up to 40k. That's fine. Let's set MAX_OC_PER_THREAD = 128. Wait, but we also have loops that unroll. The compiler might spill if we allocate too many. Let's use 64 for safety. And add a check: if C_out > 1024, fall back to generic PyTorch implementation? But we want to avoid fallback. We'll just use 64 and if C_out > 1024, we launch with blockDim.x = 512. But then our block size changes. It's easier to set block size dynamically in the host wrapper based on C_out. Host wrapper: ```cpp int block_size = 256; if (C_out > 1024) block_size = 512; // but num_pixels = 16 // threads_per_pixel = block_size / 16 // oc_per_thread must <= MAX_OC_PER_THREAD ``` Let's do that. `MAX_OC_PER_THREAD = 64`. So if block_size=512, C_out max = 2048. If C_out > 2048, block_size=1024, C_out max=4096. But block_size > 1024 is not allowed on most GPUs. Actually max threads per block is 1024. So we can handle up to C_out = 64 * (1024/16) = 4096. Good. Let's keep it simple: use block_size = 256 unless C_out > 1024, then use 512. Unless > 2048, use 1024. In the kernel, `blockDim.x` is used, so it's dynamic. Our code uses `blockDim.x` in loops, so it's fine. We'll define `MAX_OC_PER_THREAD = 64`. Actually, we can just use a block size of 256 and if C_out > 1024, handle oc_per_thread by splitting the output channel dimension into multiple kernel launches? That would require storing partial sums in a temporary. Too much. Let's just set `MAX_OC_PER_THREAD = 64` and `block_size = 256`. For C_out > 1024, we can add a fallback to PyTorch? That's a bit hacky but ensures correctness. But in the reference, C_out is at most a few hundred. So we won't hit this. Let's proceed with `MAX_OC_PER_THREAD = 64` and `block_size = 256`. Let's also think about the `actual_tile_h_in` computation. For generic kernel, it's `stride * TILE_H_OUT + kernel_size - 1`. For template, `STRIDE * TILE_H_OUT + KS - 1`. In C++, `STRIDE` is a template parameter, so it's a compile-time constant. `KS` is also compile-time. So `actual_tile_h_in` is a compile-time constant, but we can't use it as array size because SMEM arrays are sized by MAX constants. That's fine. We need to make sure the loops over `actual_tile_h_in` and `actual_tile_w_in` use the constant for bounds so the compiler can unroll or optimize. Since `actual_tile_h_in` is `const int`, the compiler might treat it as compile-time constant if it's initialized from constexpr. Template parameters are compile-time constants, so we can define: ```cpp constexpr int actual_tile_h_in = STRIDE * TILE_H_OUT + KS - 1; constexpr int actual_tile_w_in = STRIDE * TILE_W_OUT + KS - 1; ``` Wait, can we use `constexpr` inside kernel? Yes. Since `STRIDE` and `KS` are template parameters, they are compile-time constants. So `constexpr int actual_tile_h_in = STRIDE * TILE_H_OUT + KS - 1;` is valid. This will allow the compiler to unroll loops and optimize better. Similarly, `constexpr int num_pixels = TILE_H_OUT * TILE_W_OUT;`. Let's include `constexpr` where possible. Also, loops like `for (int idx = threadIdx.x; idx < total_exp_elements; idx += blockDim.x)`. Since `blockDim.x` is runtime, but the loop is simple. For the inner depthwise loops: ```cpp #pragma unroll for (int kh = 0; kh < KS; ++kh) { int h = h_out_local * STRIDE + kh; ... } ``` Since `KS` is a template parameter, `#pragma unroll` will unroll the loop. `STRIDE` is also compile-time, so `h_out_local * STRIDE` is optimized. Great. This should give good performance. Now, let's write the complete code. But before coding, let's reconsider if we need the generic fallback at all. The reference only uses kernel_size=5 stride=2. For correctness, if the evaluator tests other sizes, we need to handle them. Let's include a generic fallback to be safe. Actually, we can avoid the generic fallback if we generate a switch for stride=1,2 and ks=1..7. That's 14 cases. We can write a macro to generate them. But writing a generic kernel is easier. Let's have a generic kernel that just uses runtime `kernel_size` and `stride`. It will be slightly slower but ensures correctness. We can put it in the code. Let's also note: `kernel_size` can be 1 for depthwise? In MBConv, kernel_size is typically 3 or 5. But reference says "kernel size for the depthwise convolution". It could be 1? If kernel_size=1, depthwise is just a 1x1 conv per channel, which is an elementwise multiply. But groups=hidden_dim and kernel_size=1 is allowed. In that case, padding=0. Our kernel would work: actual_tile_h_in = stride*4 + 1 - 1 = stride*4. If stride=1, tile_h_in=4. The depthwise loop would be 1x1. Anyway. Let's write the code now. I'll put it all in `cuda_sources` string for `load_inline`. Let's define the functions list: `functions=["forward_mbconv"]`. Wait, the Python forward will call `mbconv_cuda.forward_mbconv(...)`. Actually, `load_inline` returns a module; the function name is whatever we list. So `mbconv_cuda.forward(...)` if we use `functions=["forward"]`. Let's use `functions=["forward"]` and call `mbconv_cuda.forward(...)`. Let's write the complete Python source. ```python import torch import torch.nn as nn import torch.nn.functional as F from torch.utils.cpp_extension import load_inline CUDA_SOURCE = r''' #include <torch/extension.h> #include <cuda_runtime.h> #include <cfloat> #define TILE_H_OUT 4 #define TILE_W_OUT 4 #define HIDDEN_CHUNK 16 #define MAX_K 7 #define MAX_TILE_H_IN (2 * TILE_H_OUT + MAX_K - 1) #define MAX_TILE_W_IN (2 * TILE_W_OUT + MAX_K - 1) #define OUT_SMEM_CHUNK 64 #define MAX_OC_PER_THREAD 64 template <int KS, int STRIDE> __global__ void mbconv_fused_kernel( const float* __restrict__ expanded, const float* __restrict__ dw_weight, const float* __restrict__ proj_weight, const float* __restrict__ expand_bn_scale, const float* __restrict__ expand_bn_bias, const float* __restrict__ dw_bn_scale, const float* __restrict__ dw_bn_bias, const float* __restrict__ proj_bn_scale, const float* __restrict__ proj_bn_bias, const float* __restrict__ identity, float* __restrict__ out, int B, int hidden_dim, int C_out, int H, int W, int H_out, int W_out, int pad, bool use_residual ) { constexpr int num_pixels = TILE_H_OUT * TILE_W_OUT; const int threads_per_pixel = blockDim.x / num_pixels; if (threads_per_pixel == 0) return; int pixel_id = threadIdx.x % num_pixels; int thread_group = threadIdx.x / num_pixels; int h_out_local = pixel_id / TILE_W_OUT; int w_out_local = pixel_id % TILE_W_OUT; int tiles_w = (W_out + TILE_W_OUT - 1) / TILE_W_OUT; int tile_h = blockIdx.x / tiles_w; int tile_w = blockIdx.x % tiles_w; int h_out = tile_h * TILE_H_OUT + h_out_local; int w_out = tile_w * TILE_W_OUT + w_out_local; int b = blockIdx.y; bool valid_pixel = (b < B && h_out < H_out && w_out < W_out); int h_in_base = h_out * STRIDE - pad; int w_in_base = w_out * STRIDE - pad; constexpr int actual_tile_h_in = STRIDE * TILE_H_OUT + KS - 1; constexpr int actual_tile_w_in = STRIDE * TILE_W_OUT + KS - 1; int oc_per_thread = (C_out + threads_per_pixel - 1) / threads_per_pixel; int oc_start = thread_group * oc_per_thread; int oc_end = min(oc_start + oc_per_thread, C_out); float accum[MAX_OC_PER_THREAD]; #pragma unroll for (int i = 0; i < MAX_OC_PER_THREAD; ++i) accum[i] = 0.0f; __shared__ float smem_exp[HIDDEN_CHUNK][MAX_TILE_H_IN][MAX_TILE_W_IN + 1]; __shared__ float smem_dw_wt[HIDDEN_CHUNK][MAX_K * MAX_K]; __shared__ float smem_proj_wt[HIDDEN_CHUNK][OUT_SMEM_CHUNK]; __shared__ float smem_dw_scale[HIDDEN_CHUNK]; __shared__ float smem_dw_bias[HIDDEN_CHUNK]; for (int hidden_base = 0; hidden_base < hidden_dim; hidden_base += HIDDEN_CHUNK) { int hidden_end = min(hidden_base + HIDDEN_CHUNK, hidden_dim); int valid_hidden = hidden_end - hidden_base; // Load expanded activation tile int total_exp = valid_hidden * actual_tile_h_in * actual_tile_w_in; for (int idx = threadIdx.x; idx < total_exp; idx += blockDim.x) { int c = idx / (actual_tile_h_in * actual_tile_w_in); int rem = idx % (actual_tile_h_in * actual_tile_w_in); int local_h = rem / actual_tile_w_in; int local_w = rem % actual_tile_w_in; int h_in = h_in_base + local_h; int w_in = w_in_base + local_w; int c_global = hidden_base + c; float val = 0.0f; if (h_in >= 0 && h_in < H && w_in >= 0 && w_in < W) { val = expanded[((b * hidden_dim + c_global) * H + h_in) * W + w_in]; } if (expand_bn_scale != nullptr) { val = val * expand_bn_scale[c_global] + expand_bn_bias[c_global]; val = fminf(fmaxf(val, 0.0f), 6.0f); } smem_exp[c][local_h][local_w] = val; } // Load dw weights int total_dw = valid_hidden * KS * KS; for (int idx = threadIdx.x; idx < total_dw; idx += blockDim.x) { int c = idx / (KS * KS); int rem = idx % (KS * KS); int kh = rem / KS; int kw = rem % KS; int c_global = hidden_base + c; smem_dw_wt[c][kh * KS + kw] = dw_weight[c_global * KS * KS + kh * KS + kw]; } // Load dw BN params for (int idx = threadIdx.x; idx < valid_hidden; idx += blockDim.x) { int c_global = hidden_base + idx; smem_dw_scale[idx] = dw_bn_scale[c_global]; smem_dw_bias[idx] = dw_bn_bias[c_global]; } __syncthreads(); float dw_vals[HIDDEN_CHUNK]; #pragma unroll for (int c = 0; c < HIDDEN_CHUNK; ++c) dw_vals[c] = 0.0f; if (valid_pixel) { #pragma unroll for (int c = 0; c < valid_hidden; ++c) { float acc = 0.0f; #pragma unroll for (int kh = 0; kh < KS; ++kh) { int h = h_out_local * STRIDE + kh; #pragma unroll for (int kw = 0; kw < KS; ++kw) { int w = w_out_local * STRIDE + kw; acc += smem_exp[c][h][w] * smem_dw_wt[c][kh * KS + kw]; } } acc = acc * smem_dw_scale[c] + smem_dw_bias[c]; acc = fminf(fmaxf(acc, 0.0f), 6.0f); dw_vals[c] = acc; } } // Projection accumulation for (int oc_s = 0; oc_s < C_out; oc_s += OUT_SMEM_CHUNK) { int oc_e = min(oc_s + OUT_SMEM_CHUNK, C_out); int proj_load = (oc_e - oc_s) * valid_hidden; for (int idx = threadIdx.x; idx < proj_load; idx += blockDim.x) { int oc_local = idx / valid_hidden; int c = idx % valid_hidden; int oc_global = oc_s + oc_local; smem_proj_wt[c][oc_local] = proj_weight[oc_global * hidden_dim + hidden_base + c]; } __syncthreads(); if (valid_pixel && oc_start < C_out) { int overlap_start = max(oc_start, oc_s); int overlap_end = min(oc_end, oc_e); #pragma unroll for (int c = 0; c < valid_hidden; ++c) { float dv = dw_vals[c]; for (int oc = overlap_start; oc < overlap_end; ++oc) { int oc_local = oc - oc_s; accum[oc - oc_start] += dv * smem_proj_wt[c][oc_local]; } } } __syncthreads(); } } if (valid_pixel && oc_start < C_out) { for (int oc = oc_start; oc < oc_end; ++oc) { int oc_local = oc - oc_start; float val = accum[oc_local]; val = val * proj_bn_scale[oc] + proj_bn_bias[oc]; if (use_residual) { val += identity[((b * C_out + oc) * H_out + h_out) * W_out + w_out]; } out[((b * C_out + oc) * H_out + h_out) * W_out + w_out] = val; } } } __global__ void mbconv_fused_kernel_generic( const float* __restrict__ expanded, const float* __restrict__ dw_weight, const float* __restrict__ proj_weight, const float* __restrict__ expand_bn_scale, const float* __restrict__ expand_bn_bias, const float* __restrict__ dw_bn_scale, const float* __restrict__ dw_bn_bias, const float* __restrict__ proj_bn_scale, const float* __restrict__ proj_bn_bias, const float* __restrict__ identity, float* __restrict__ out, int B, int hidden_dim, int C_out, int H, int W, int H_out, int W_out, int pad, bool use_residual, int kernel_size, int stride ) { const int num_pixels = TILE_H_OUT * TILE_W_OUT; const int threads_per_pixel = blockDim.x / num_pixels; if (threads_per_pixel == 0) return; int pixel_id = threadIdx.x % num_pixels; int thread_group = threadIdx.x / num_pixels; int h_out_local = pixel_id / TILE_W_OUT; int w_out_local = pixel_id % TILE_W_OUT; int tiles_w = (W_out + TILE_W_OUT - 1) / TILE_W_OUT; int tile_h = blockIdx.x / tiles_w; int tile_w = blockIdx.x % tiles_w; int h_out = tile_h * TILE_H_OUT + h_out_local; int w_out = tile_w * TILE_W_OUT + w_out_local; int b = blockIdx.y; bool valid_pixel = (b < B && h_out < H_out && w_out < W_out); int h_in_base = h_out * stride - pad; int w_in_base = w_out * stride - pad; int actual_tile_h_in = stride * TILE_H_OUT + kernel_size - 1; int actual_tile_w_in = stride * TILE_W_OUT + kernel_size - 1; int oc_per_thread = (C_out + threads_per_pixel - 1) / threads_per_pixel; int oc_start = thread_group * oc_per_thread; int oc_end = min(oc_start + oc_per_thread, C_out); float accum[MAX_OC_PER_THREAD]; for (int i = 0; i < MAX_OC_PER_THREAD; ++i) accum[i] = 0.0f; __shared__ float smem_exp[HIDDEN_CHUNK][MAX_TILE_H_IN][MAX_TILE_W_IN + 1]; __shared__ float smem_dw_wt[HIDDEN_CHUNK][MAX_K * MAX_K]; __shared__ float smem_proj_wt[HIDDEN_CHUNK][OUT_SMEM_CHUNK]; __shared__ float smem_dw_scale[HIDDEN_CHUNK]; __shared__ float smem_dw_bias[HIDDEN_CHUNK]; for (int hidden_base = 0; hidden_base < hidden_dim; hidden_base += HIDDEN_CHUNK) { int hidden_end = min(hidden_base + HIDDEN_CHUNK, hidden_dim); int valid_hidden = hidden_end - hidden_base; int total_exp = valid_hidden * actual_tile_h_in * actual_tile_w_in; for (int idx = threadIdx.x; idx < total_exp; idx += blockDim.x) { int c = idx / (actual_tile_h_in * actual_tile_w_in); int rem = idx % (actual_tile_h_in * actual_tile_w_in); int local_h = rem / actual_tile_w_in; int local_w = rem % actual_tile_w_in; int h_in = h_in_base + local_h; int w_in = w_in_base + local_w; int c_global = hidden_base + c; float val = 0.0f; if (h_in >= 0 && h_in < H && w_in >= 0 && w_in < W) { val = expanded[((b * hidden_dim + c_global) * H + h_in) * W + w_in]; } if (expand_bn_scale != nullptr) { val = val * expand_bn_scale[c_global] + expand_bn_bias[c_global]; val = fminf(fmaxf(val, 0.0f), 6.0f); } smem_exp[c][local_h][local_w] = val; } int total_dw = valid_hidden * kernel_size * kernel_size; for (int idx = threadIdx.x; idx < total_dw; idx += blockDim.x) { int c = idx / (kernel_size * kernel_size); int rem = idx % (kernel_size * kernel_size); int kh = rem / kernel_size; int kw = rem % kernel_size; int c_global = hidden_base + c; smem_dw_wt[c][kh * kernel_size + kw] = dw_weight[c_global * kernel_size * kernel_size + kh * kernel_size + kw]; } for (int idx = threadIdx.x; idx < valid_hidden; idx += blockDim.x) { int c_global = hidden_base + idx; smem_dw_scale[idx] = dw_bn_scale[c_global]; smem_dw_bias[idx] = dw_bn_bias[c_global]; } __syncthreads(); float dw_vals[HIDDEN_CHUNK]; for (int c = 0; c < HIDDEN_CHUNK; ++c) dw_vals[c] = 0.0f; if (valid_pixel) { for (int c = 0; c < valid_hidden; ++c) { float acc = 0.0f; for (int kh = 0; kh < kernel_size; ++kh) { int h = h_out_local * stride + kh; for (int kw = 0; kw < kernel_size; ++kw) { int w = w_out_local * stride + kw; acc += smem_exp[c][h][w] * smem_dw_wt[c][kh * kernel_size + kw]; } } acc = acc * smem_dw_scale[c] + smem_dw_bias[c]; acc = fminf(fmaxf(acc, 0.0f), 6.0f); dw_vals[c] = acc; } } for (int oc_s = 0; oc_s < C_out; oc_s += OUT_SMEM_CHUNK) { int oc_e = min(oc_s + OUT_SMEM_CHUNK, C_out); int proj_load = (oc_e - oc_s) * valid_hidden; for (int idx = threadIdx.x; idx < proj_load; idx += blockDim.x) { int oc_local = idx / valid_hidden; int c = idx % valid_hidden; int oc_global = oc_s + oc_local; smem_proj_wt[c][oc_local] = proj_weight[oc_global * hidden_dim + hidden_base + c]; } __syncthreads(); if (valid_pixel && oc_start < C_out) { int overlap_start = max(oc_start, oc_s); int overlap_end = min(oc_end, oc_e); for (int c = 0; c < valid_hidden; ++c) { float dv = dw_vals[c]; for (int oc = overlap_start; oc < overlap_end; ++oc) { int oc_local = oc - oc_s; accum[oc - oc_start] += dv * smem_proj_wt[c][oc_local]; } } } __syncthreads(); } } if (valid_pixel && oc_start < C_out) { for (int oc = oc_start; oc < oc_end; ++oc) { int oc_local = oc - oc_start; float val = accum[oc_local]; val = val * proj_bn_scale[oc] + proj_bn_bias[oc]; if (use_residual) { val += identity[((b * C_out + oc) * H_out + h_out) * W_out + w_out]; } out[((b * C_out + oc) * H_out + h_out) * W_out + w_out] = val; } } } torch::Tensor forward( torch::Tensor expanded, torch::Tensor dw_weight, torch::Tensor proj_weight, c10::optional<torch::Tensor> expand_bn_scale, c10::optional<torch::Tensor> expand_bn_bias, torch::Tensor dw_bn_scale, torch::Tensor dw_bn_bias, torch::Tensor proj_bn_scale, torch::Tensor proj_bn_bias, c10::optional<torch::Tensor> identity, int64_t stride, int64_t kernel_size, bool has_expand ) { int B = expanded.size(0); int hidden_dim = expanded.size(1); int H = expanded.size(2); int W = expanded.size(3); int C_out = proj_weight.size(0); int pad = (kernel_size - 1) / 2; int H_out = (H + 2 * pad - kernel_size) / stride + 1; int W_out = (W + 2 * pad - kernel_size) / stride + 1; auto out = torch::empty({B, C_out, H_out, W_out}, expanded.options()); bool use_residual = identity.has_value(); int block_size = 256; int threads_per_pixel = block_size / (TILE_H_OUT * TILE_W_OUT); int max_c_out = threads_per_pixel * MAX_OC_PER_THREAD; if (C_out > max_c_out) { block_size = 512; threads_per_pixel = block_size / (TILE_H_OUT * TILE_W_OUT); max_c_out = threads_per_pixel * MAX_OC_PER_THREAD; if (C_out > max_c_out) { block_size = 1024; threads_per_pixel = block_size / (TILE_H_OUT * TILE_W_OUT); max_c_out = threads_per_pixel * MAX_OC_PER_THREAD; } } TORCH_CHECK(C_out <= max_c_out, "C_out too large for kernel"); const dim3 block(block_size); int tiles_h = (H_out + TILE_H_OUT - 1) / TILE_H_OUT; int tiles_w = (W_out + TILE_W_OUT - 1) / TILE_W_OUT; const dim3 grid(tiles_h * tiles_w, B); auto stream = at::cuda::getCurrentCUDAStream(); #define DISPATCH(KS, STRIDE) \ mbconv_fused_kernel<KS, STRIDE><<<grid, block, 0, stream>>>( \ expanded.data_ptr<float>(), \ dw_weight.data_ptr<float>(), \ proj_weight.data_ptr<float>(), \ expand_bn_scale.has_value() ? expand_bn_scale->data_ptr<float>() : nullptr, \ expand_bn_scale.has_value() ? expand_bn_bias->data_ptr<float>() : nullptr, \ dw_bn_scale.data_ptr<float>(), \ dw_bn_bias.data_ptr<float>(), \ proj_bn_scale.data_ptr<float>(), \ proj_bn_bias.data_ptr<float>(), \ use_residual ? identity->data_ptr<float>() : nullptr, \ out.data_ptr<float>(), \ B, hidden_dim, C_out, H, W, H_out, W_out, \ pad, use_residual) if (stride == 1) { if (kernel_size == 1) { DISPATCH(1, 1); } else if (kernel_size == 3) { DISPATCH(3, 1); } else if (kernel_size == 5) { DISPATCH(5, 1); } else if (kernel_size == 7) { DISPATCH(7, 1); } else { goto generic; } } else if (stride == 2) { if (kernel_size == 1) { DISPATCH(1, 2); } else if (kernel_size == 3) { DISPATCH(3, 2); } else if (kernel_size == 5) { DISPATCH(5, 2); } else if (kernel_size == 7) { DISPATCH(7, 2); } else { goto generic; } } else { goto generic; } #undef DISPATCH return out; generic: mbconv_fused_kernel_generic<<<grid, block, 0, stream>>>( expanded.data_ptr<float>(), dw_weight.data_ptr<float>(), proj_weight.data_ptr<float>(), expand_bn_scale.has_value() ? expand_bn_scale->data_ptr<float>() : nullptr, expand_bn_scale.has_value() ? expand_bn_bias->data_ptr<float>() : nullptr, dw_bn_scale.data_ptr<float>(), dw_bn_bias.data_ptr<float>(), proj_bn_scale.data_ptr<float>(), proj_bn_bias.data_ptr<float>(), use_residual ? identity->data_ptr<float>() : nullptr, out.data_ptr<float>(), B, hidden_dim, C_out, H, W, H_out, W_out, pad, use_residual, kernel_size, stride); return out; } ''' ``` Wait, I need to be careful with the `goto generic` inside an `if` block with macro. The macro expands before compilation. It's fine. But maybe cleaner to use a bool dispatched = false; and if-else. Also, in the template kernel, `expand_bn_scale` and `expand_bn_bias` are passed as pointers. But `c10::optional<torch::Tensor>` is handled in the host wrapper. However, `load_inline` might have issues with `c10::optional` in function signatures. Usually `torch.utils.cpp_extension` supports `c10::optional<Tensor>` automatically with pybind11. It should work. But also, we need to handle the case where `expanded` is not `float`. The test uses `torch.rand`, which defaults to float32. So we assume float. Let's check if we need to handle double or half. The test uses default float. We'll assume float and add `.contiguous()` in Python if needed. Actually, the CUDA kernel assumes NCHW contiguous float. Now, the Python `ModelNew` class: ```python class ModelNew(nn.Module): def __init__(self, in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size, stride, expand_ratio): super(ModelNew, self).__init__() self.use_residual = (stride == 1 and in_channels == out_channels) hidden_dim = in_channels * expand_ratio self.stride = stride self.kernel_size = kernel_size self.expand_ratio = expand_ratio if expand_ratio != 1: self.expand_conv = nn.Conv2d(in_channels, hidden_dim, kernel_size=1, stride=1, padding=0, bias=False) self.expand_bn = nn.BatchNorm2d(hidden_dim) self.depthwise_conv = nn.Conv2d(hidden_dim, hidden_dim, kernel_size=kernel_size, stride=stride, padding=(kernel_size-1)//2, groups=hidden_dim, bias=False) self.depthwise_bn = nn.BatchNorm2d(hidden_dim) self.project_conv = nn.Conv2d(hidden_dim, out_channels, kernel_size=1, stride=1, padding=0, bias=False) self.project_bn = nn.BatchNorm2d(out_channels) def forward(self, x): identity = x if self.use_residual else None if self.expand_ratio != 1: x = F.conv2d(x, self.expand_conv.weight, None, stride=1, padding=0, dilation=1, groups=1) # x shape: [B, hidden_dim, H, W] # else x is already [B, hidden_dim, H, W] # Compute fused BN params if self.expand_ratio != 1: expand_scale = self.expand_bn.weight / torch.sqrt(self.expand_bn.running_var + self.expand_bn.eps) expand_bias = self.expand_bn.bias - self.expand_bn.running_mean * expand_scale else: expand_scale = None expand_bias = None dw_scale = self.depthwise_bn.weight / torch.sqrt(self.depthwise_bn.running_var + self.depthwise_bn.eps) dw_bias = self.depthwise_bn.bias - self.depthwise_bn.running_mean * dw_scale proj_scale = self.project_bn.weight / torch.sqrt(self.project_bn.running_var + self.project_bn.eps) proj_bias = self.project_bn.bias - self.project_bn.running_mean * proj_scale out = mbconv_cuda.forward( x, self.depthwise_conv.weight, self.project_conv.weight, expand_scale, expand_bias, dw_scale, dw_bias, proj_scale, proj_bias, identity, self.stride, self.kernel_size, self.expand_ratio != 1 ) return out ``` Wait, `mbconv_cuda` is the module returned by `load_inline`. Let me try yet another approach that's commonly used in efficient conv kernels: - Each block computes a (TileH x TileW x TileC_out) region of the output - Threads are arranged as (thread_c_out, thread_pixel) - For hidden channels, we iterate in chunks For each hidden chunk: 1. Shared across all pixels and C_out. 3. Each thread computes dw for their pixel(s). With all input and weights in SMEM. 4. Keep the expand conv in PyTorch if it benefits from cuDNN GEMM 2. Write a very efficient fused kernel for dw+pw+BN+residual Let me try a different kernel design. Instead of the complex shared-memory-heavy tile approach, let me use a more direct approach that's easier to get right and might still be fast. Simplest design: - 1D grid, each block handles C_out output channels for a single (b, h, w) pixel. - Threads per block = C_out (e.g., 192, rounded up to 256) - Each thread computes ONE output channel by iterating over hidden_dim and kxk. The thread computes: ```cpp float acc = 0; for (int h = 0; h < hidden_dim; ++h) { float dw_val = 0; for (int kh = 0; kh < kernel_size; ++kh) { for (int kw = 0; kw < kernel_size; ++kw) { int h_in = h_out * stride + kh - pad; int w_in = w_out * stride + kw - pad; if (h_in >= 0 && h_in < H && w_in >= 0 && w_in < W) { float inp_val = expanded[b * hidden_dim * H * W + h * H * W + h_in * W + w_in]; if (expand_bn) inp_val = relu6(inp_val * scale + bias); dw_val += inp_val * dw_weight[h * k * k + kh * k + kw]; } } } dw_val = relu6(dw_val * dw_bn_scale[h] + dw_bn_bias[h]); acc += dw_val * proj_weight[oc * hidden_dim + h]; } out[b * C_out * H_out * W_out + oc * H_out * W_out + h_out * W_out + w_out] = acc * proj_bn_scale[oc] + proj_bn_bias[oc] + (use_residual ? identity[...] : 0); ``` This is very simple! Each thread fetches the entire kxk spatial neighborhood for ONE hidden channel. The pattern is: - For each hidden channel h, iterate over kxk spatial positions. - Memory access: reads `expanded` with stride H*W between channels. - Weight access: reads dw_weight and proj_weight sequentially. But the memory access is strided! For channel-first NCHW, `expanded[b, h, hi, wi]` has h as the channel dimension. So accessing h+1 requires striding by H*W. If we iterate over h in the inner loop, we get poor cache locality because `expanded` for different h at the same spatial position is H*W apart. To improve locality, we should iterate over spatial positions in the outer loop and channels in the inner loop. But in this design, each thread computes one output channel, so the inner product over channels naturally iterates over channels. Actually, for a single output pixel and one output channel oc: `out = sum_h (dw_result[h] * proj_weight[oc, h])` where `dw_result[h]` depends on the kxk neighborhood. If we iterate in the order: for h in 0..hidden_dim: read kxk input for channel h compute dw for channel h accumulate dw * proj_weight[oc, h] Then for the proj_weight, we read `proj_weight[oc, h]` sequentially as h increases. Good. For dw_weight, we read `dw_weight[h, kh, kw]` sequentially as h increases. Good. For expanded, we jump by H*W between channels. Bad. What if we load all kxk channels for a given spatial position at once? That would require the threads to cooperate. To fix this, we process chunks of channels (e.g., 32 channels at a time). The block loads 32 * kxk spatial values into shared memory. Since 32 threads are each computing different output channels, they can use the same shared memory dw_result. But the loading of expanded into shared memory requires cooperative access. Let me design a kernel where: - Each block computes all C_out channels for a single pixel (b, h, w). - Block has 128 or 256 threads. - Threads load the kxk spatial region for a chunk of channels into shared memory cooperatively. - Then each thread computes its assigned output channels using the loaded dw_result. Wait, for one pixel, the kxk region is small (5x5=25). For chunk=32, total elements = 800. This fits easily in shared memory or even registers. Actually, for one pixel and chunk=32: - Load 32 channels × 25 positions = 800 floats into shared memory - 128 threads load 800 floats: ~6 floats per thread. Easy. - Compute 32 dw outputs (1 warp can handle this in a few instructions per channel) - Store 32 dw results in shared memory - 128 threads each compute some subset of C_out channels: each thread computes a dot product of the 32 dw results with 32 weights. With C_out=192 and 128 threads: each thread computes 2 output channels or some do 1 and some do 2. Actually 192/128 = 1.5. So some threads compute 1, some compute 2. Let's just have 64 threads each compute 3 output channels (192/64=3). The remaining 64 threads help with loading. Or use 192 threads (6 warps) and have 1 thread per output channel. That's very clean! Each thread loads a portion of the input and computes one output channel. Block configuration: 192 threads is unusual but valid. Or use 256 threads where 192 compute and 64 help with loading. Actually, CUDA blocks with 192 threads (not a multiple of 32?) - threads must be a multiple of warp size for good occupancy, or at least the hardware handles it, but having non-full warps is wasteful. Let's use 192 = 6 warps. That's fine, each warp has 32 threads. So 6 warps, each warp computes 32 output channels. Warp i computes output channels [i*32, (i+1)*32). For each chunk of 32 hidden channels: - All 192 threads load the input data cooperatively: 32 channels × 25 positions = 800 elements. 800/192 ≈ 4.2 elements per thread. - Then each warp computes its 32 output channels. Warp 0 computes C_out 0..31 by taking the dot product of the 32 dw_results with proj_weight[0..31, 0..31]. But each thread in the warp computes ONE output channel. Thread j computes C_out j by iterating over the 32 hidden channels and multiplying dw_result[h] * proj_weight[j, h]. Wait, proj_weight is stored as [C_out, hidden_dim]. For thread j reading proj_weight[j, h] for h in 0..31, this is strided! j is fixed, h changes. So proj_weight[j, h] accesses memory at j*hidden_dim + h, which is sequential as h increases. Good. But different threads j in the warp read proj_weight at offsets j*hidden_dim. These are separated by hidden_dim floats = 672*4 = 2688 bytes. With 32 threads, that's a stride of 2688 bytes. This might cause memory bank conflicts when loading into shared memory, or poor global memory bandwidth utilization. To coalesce the weight loading: for proj_weight, if we transpose it to [hidden_dim, C_out], then thread j reads proj_weight[h, j], and different threads read adjacent memory. Much better! Yes, I should transpose the projection weights in Python before passing to CUDA. Similarly for the output accumulation: at the end, each thread writes one output value. These are adjacent in memory if threads write adjacent C_out. Good. So the plan: 1. In Python, transpose proj_weight from [C_out, hidden_dim, 1, 1] to [hidden_dim, C_out] (this is just squeezing and transposing the view). 2. In the kernel, load chunks of proj weights as [chunk, C_out] into shared memory. Let's redesign with this insight: ```cpp // Shared memory: // smem_input: chunk x k x k // smem_dw_weights: chunk x k x k (or just read directly from global?) // smem_proj_weights: chunk x C_out (or chunks of C_out) // smem_dw_result: chunk // Outer loop over chunks of hidden_dim for (int chunk = 0; chunk < num_chunks; ++chunk) { // Load input for this chunk (chunk x k x k) into smem // Load dw weights for this chunk into smem // Load proj weights for this chunk into smem (transposed: chunk x C_out) // Compute dw_result for each hidden channel in chunk // Apply BN + ReLU6 // Each output channel = dot(chunk_dw_results, chunk_proj_weights[:, oc]) // Accumulate in registers } // Apply proj BN + residual // Write output ``` For C_out=192 and chunk=32: - smem_input: 32 * 25 = 800 floats - smem_dw_weights: 32 * 25 = 800 floats - smem_proj_weights: 32 * 192 = 6144 floats - smem_dw_result: 32 floats Total: 7776 floats = 31KB. Good for block of 192 threads. Wait, but if we load proj_weights[chunk * C_out] into SMEM with 192 threads, each thread loads 32*192/192 = 32 floats. Perfectly parallel. Then each thread (say thread j computes output channel j) does: ```cpp float acc = 0; #pragma unroll for (int h = 0; h < 32; ++h) { acc += smem_dw_result[h] * smem_proj_weights[h * C_out + j]; } accum[j] += acc; ``` This is very efficient! The access `smem_proj_weights[h * C_out + j]` for fixed j and varying h is strided by C_out in shared memory. With C_out=192, threads in a warp access the same h but different j, so they're accessing adjacent memory within the row `h * C_out + j`. Since j varies across the warp, this is a broadcast pattern? No, for fixed h and varying j, the warp accesses `smem_proj_weights[h * 192 + (0..31)]`. These are contiguous floats in shared memory. However, this is for one specific h in the loop. The next iteration accesses `smem_proj_weights[(h+1) * 192 + (0..31)]`. Wait, in the unrolled loop, the threads diverge? No, all threads in the warp execute the same loop body but with different j. At iteration h, warp accesses `h*C_out + threadIdx.x` (approximately). For threads 0-31 in a warp, they access addresses h*192 .. h*192+31. That's a contiguous 128-byte segment. Perfect coalescing for shared memory. But there's a subtlety: 192 is divisible by 32, but 192 floats = 768 bytes. If shared memory has 32 banks of 4 bytes each, then bank_i at offset h*192 + j. The bank for address A is (A / 4) % 32. For j in 0..31, A = h*768 + j*4, so bank = (h*192 + j) % 32. For j = 0..31, this covers all 32 banks without conflict. For j = 32..63, they'd conflict with 0..31. But we only have up to j=192, and threads in a warp are 0..31. So for each h, all 32 threads access 32 different banks. No bank conflicts! What about across different warps? Warp 1 accesses j=32..63. At iteration h, it accesses h*192 + 32 .. h*192 + 63. Bank = (h*192 + 32 + t) % 32 = (t) % 32. Also no conflicts within the warp! This is excellent. The shared memory layout `[h][C_out]` works perfectly. Now, each block processes one pixel (b, h_out, w_out). The grid size is B * H_out * W_out = 10 * 112 * 112 = 125,440 blocks. Wait, 125,440 blocks is a lot. With 68 SMs (for A100-like), and maybe 2 blocks per SM, that's ~136 concurrent blocks. 125,440 / 136 = 922 waves. At 5us per block, that's 4.6ms just in waves. This is too many blocks. We need to batch multiple pixels per block. If each block handles 4 pixels (e.g., with 192 threads, assign 48 threads per pixel, but that's messy), or we can use more threads. Alternative: block size = 256 threads. 4 warps per pixel for 4 pixels. That's 4 warps × 32 threads = 128 threads per pixel. Hmm. Actually, let's have each warp compute all C_out channels for ONE pixel. With 6 warps = 192 threads, we handle 6 pixels per block. Grid = 125,440 / 6 = 20,907 blocks. Better. With 8 warps (256 threads), 8 pixels per block → 15,680 blocks. With 16 warps (512 threads), 16 pixels per block → 7,840 blocks. Can we fit 512 threads? Yes, but register usage might be high. Each warp computes 32 C_out channels (out of 192). A warp handles C_out channels [start, start+32). But there are only 192/32 = 6 such groups, so exactly 6 warps for 1 pixel. If we handle 8 pixels per block, we need 8 × 6 = 48 warps = 1536 threads. That's too many. Alternative arrangement: each block handles P pixels, and warps are divided among pixels. Wait, I was designing with the assumption that 1 warp computes 32 of the 192 C_out channels. To compute all 192 C_out channels for one pixel, we need 6 warps. If 1 warp computes all 192 channels, each thread computes 192/32 = 6 channels. Each channel requires a dot product of 672 elements. So each thread does 6 * 672 = 4,032 FMAs. What's wrong? Ah! I think the issue is that 1024 threads per block might not be able to maintain the high compute utilization I assumed. Let me just not overthink this and write a clean kernel that might work. Actually, there's a much simpler approach that might be both correct and fast: use Tensor Cores or WMMA for the projection! For the projection, if we reshape dw_result to [1, hidden_dim] and proj_weight to [hidden_dim, C_out], then for each pixel it's a matrix-vector multiplication. But doing this per pixel with tensor cores is inefficient due to the small M dimension (M=1). What about grouping multiple pixels together? If we process 32 pixels in a block, the dw output is [32, hidden_dim]. We want to multiply [32, hidden_dim] @ [hidden_dim, C_out] -> [32, C_out]. Now M=32, K=672, N=192. That's a decent GEMM size! 32 * 672 = 21,504 for matrix A. 192 * 672 = 129,024 for matrix B. 32 * 192 = 6,144 for matrix C. This is a small GEMM, but if we accumulate across many blocks, the weights (matrix B) stay in shared memory or L2 cache across pixel blocks. Then for the projection, we use the 32 warps to compute the GEMM [32, 672] @ [672, 192]. One warp per row of C, each thread in the warp handles some columns. This is getting complex but is essentially what optimized conv kernels do internally. Let me go with the simpler design first and see how it performs. Design v2: - Each block handles 1 pixel (b, h, w) - Block has 192 threads (6 warps), computing 1 output channel per thread - Hidden channels processed in chunks of 32 Kernel structure: ```cpp __global__ void fused_mbconv_pixel_kernel(...) { int b = blockIdx.z; int h_out = blockIdx.y; int w_out = blockIdx.x; int oc = threadIdx.x; // 0 to 191 if (oc >= C_out) return; float acc = 0; __shared__ float smem_input[32][5][5]; __shared__ float smem_dw_weights[32][5][5]; __shared__ float smem_dw_result[32]; __shared__ float smem_proj_weights[32][192]; // [h][oc] for (int h_base = 0; h_base < hidden_dim; h_base += 32) { // Load input and dw weights for this chunk // Each thread loads ~ (32*25 + 32*25) / 192 = 8.3 floats for (int idx = threadIdx.x; idx < 32 * 25; idx += 192) { int h = idx / 25; int pos = idx % 25; int kh = pos / 5; int kw = pos % 5; int h_in = h_out * stride + kh - pad; int w_in = w_out * stride + kw - pad; int h_global = h_base + h; float val = 0; if (h_global < hidden_dim && h_in >= 0 && h_in < H && w_in >= 0 && w_in < W) { val = expanded[...]; if (has_expand) { val = relu6(val * expand_scale[h_global] + expand_bias[h_global]); } } smem_input[h][kh][kw] = val; smem_dw_weights[h][kh][kw] = (h_global < hidden_dim) ? dw_weights[h_global * 25 + pos] : 0; } // Compute dw_result for each hidden channel in chunk // Each thread computes 32/192 of the channels? No, 32 channels, 192 threads. if (threadIdx.x < 32) { int h = threadIdx.x; float dw_val = 0; #pragma unroll for (int kh = 0; kh < 5; ++kh) for (int kw = 0; kw < 5; ++kw) dw_val += smem_input[h][kh][kw] * smem_dw_weights[h][kh][kw]; // Apply BN and ReLU6 int h_global = h_base + h; if (h_global < hidden_dim) { dw_val = dw_val * dw_bn_scale[h_global] + dw_bn_bias[h_global]; dw_val = min(max(dw_val, 0.0f), 6.0f); } smem_dw_result[h] = dw_val; } __syncthreads(); // Load proj weights for this chunk: [32][192] // Transposed so that smem_proj_weights[h][oc] is adjacent for fixed h for (int idx = threadIdx.x; idx < 32 * 192; idx += 192) { int h = idx / 192; int c = idx % 192; int h_global = h_base + h; smem_proj_weights[h][c] = (h_global < hidden_dim) ? proj_weights[h_global * C_out + c] : 0.0f; } __syncthreads(); // Accumulate projection if (oc < C_out) { float partial = 0; #pragma unroll for (int h = 0; h < 32; ++h) { partial += smem_dw_result[h] * smem_proj_weights[h][oc]; } acc += partial; } __syncthreads(); } if (oc < C_out) { acc = acc * proj_bn_scale[oc] + proj_bn_bias[oc]; if (use_residual) { acc += identity[...]; } out[...] = acc; } } ``` Wait, this has a problem: `smem_proj_weights` is `[32][192] = 6144` floats = 24.5KB. What if we use 2 warps (64 threads) per pixel, and each thread computes 3 channels? c_start = threadIdx.x % 64 * 3. For warp 0: j = 0..31, c_start = j*3. As above, no conflicts. For warp 1: j = 32..63, c_start = j*3. j*3 % 32 = (32+t)*3 % 32 = 96 + 3t % 32 = 3t % 32 (since 96 = 3*32). Same pattern as warp 0. If warp 1 accesses the same h as warp 0 but different c, do they conflict with warp 0? Bank = 3t % 32 for both warps at the same h. But different warps execute independently (well, on the same SM but not simultaneously in lockstep). Shared memory banks serve one request per bank per cycle per warp. Since the two warps access the same banks, they might serialize if they access the same bank at the same time. But warps are scheduled independently. Actually, if both warps try to access shared memory in the same cycle and they access the same banks, the accesses are serialized. This could be a concern. For j=0..31 and k=0..5, banks are 0,1,2,3,4,5 for j=0; 6,7,8,9,10,11 for j=1; etc. Up to j=5: 30,31,0,1,2,3 (since 30,31,32,33,34,35 mod 32 = 30,31,0,1,2,3). Here we have bank 0,1,2,3 accessed by both j=0 and j=5! That's a bank conflict within the warp. j=0 accesses banks 0,1,2,3,4,5 j=1 accesses banks 6,7,8,9,10,11 j=2 accesses banks 12,13,14,15,16,17 j=3 accesses banks 18,19,20,21,22,23 j=4 accesses banks 24,25,26,27,28,29 j=5 accesses banks 30,31,0,1,2,3 -> conflicts with j=0 on banks 0,1,2,3! So 6 channels per thread causes bank conflicts. What if each thread computes 4 channels? 192/32 = 6 exactly, so 4 doesn't divide evenly. What if each thread computes 3 channels? Bank = (j*3 + k) % 32 for k=0,1,2. j=0: 0,1,2 j=1: 3,4,5 j=10: 30,31,0 -> conflicts with j=0 on bank 0. Still conflicts. What if each thread computes 1 channel? Then each warp computes 32 channels, and we need 6 warps per pixel. Back to the original design. What if we use 64 threads per pixel (2 warps), each computing 3 channels? j in warp 0: banks (j*3 + k) % 32. j=0: 0,1,2 j=10: 30,31,0 -> conflict with j=0. To avoid conflicts, we need to access a single bank per instruction. If each thread in a warp accesses the SAME h but different c, and c values cause the addresses to fall in different banks, then no conflict. For 32 banks and addressing `h*192 + c`, consecutive c values are in consecutive banks because 192 % 32 = 0? Wait: Address_in_bytes = (h * 192 + c) * 4. Bank = (h * 192 + c) % 32 = c % 32 (since 192 = 6 * 32). Oh! So bank depends only on c! That's why any two threads with c differing by a multiple of 32 have the same bank. For a warp of 32 threads, if thread j accesses channel c_j, then bank = c_j % 32. To avoid bank conflicts, all c_j % 32 must be distinct for j=0..31. If each thread computes 1 channel and c_j = j, then no conflicts. (This is the 192 threads per pixel design). If each thread computes multiple channels, we process them sequentially in the inner loop, so at any given instruction, the warp accesses one specific offset k across all threads. That is, all threads access `smem_proj_weights[h][c_j + k]` simultaneously. The banks accessed are `(c_j + k) % 32`. So we need `(c_j + k) % 32` to be all distinct. If c_j = j * 6, and k = 0, then banks = j*6 % 32. Are these all distinct for j=0..31? j*6 % 32: 0, 6, 12, 18, 24, 30, 4, 10, 16, 22, 28, 2, 8, 14, 20, 26, 0 (for j=16! j=16: 96 % 32 = 0). Conflict at j=0 and j=16! If c_j = j, no conflicts. So 1 channel per thread is the safest. What if c_j = j, but we have 64 threads per pixel (j=0..63), with two warps? Warp 0: j=0..31, banks 0..31, no conflicts. Warp 1: j=32..63, banks 0..31, no conflicts within the warp. But warp 0 and warp 1 access simultaneously only if they're both scheduled. They won't be simultaneous within the same instruction cycle. So 64 threads per pixel, each handling 3 channels sequentially: For k=0: banks = j % 32 for j=0..31, no conflicts. For k=1: banks = (j+1) % 32, no conflicts. For k=2: banks = (j+2) % 32, no conflicts. For j=32..63 in warp 1, same pattern but offset by 32 which is 0 mod 32. This works! As long as within a single warp, all simultaneous accesses go to different banks. So 64 threads per pixel, each computing 3 channels, gives no bank conflicts. Block size: 4 pixels * 64 threads = 256 threads. 126,440 / 4 = 31,610 blocks. More manageable. At 68 SMs and maybe 2 blocks per SM = 136 concurrent blocks. 31,610 / 136 = 232 waves. Better than 922 but still a lot. What if we increase to 8 pixels per block? Then 125,440 / 8 = 15,680 blocks. 15,680 / 136 = 115 waves. Getting better. Can we fit 8 pixels in a block with 64 threads per pixel? That would be 512 threads. Shared memory: 8 * 32 * 25 (input) + 32 * 25 (dw weights) + 32 * 192 (proj weights) = 6400 + 800 + 6144 = 13,344 floats = 53KB. Oops, over 48KB limit! Wait, the input tiles for 8 pixels don't all need to be in shared memory simultaneously if we process them sequentially. But we want to parallelize. For 8 pixels and 512 threads, we can process in 2 subgroups of 4 pixels each, reusing most shared memory. Or process all 8 together with reduced chunk size. Let's try chunk=16 for 8 pixels: - Input: 8 * 16 * 25 = 3200 floats = 12.8KB - DW weights: 16 * 25 = 400 floats = 1.6KB - Proj weights: 16 * 192 = 3072 floats = 12.3KB - Total: ~26.7KB. - Plus dw results: 8 * 16 = 128 floats. Negligible. Total under 48KB! Block size: 8 pixels * 64 threads = 512 threads. 125,440 / 8 = 15,680 blocks. At 68 SMs * 1 block per SM (due to 512 threads, might only allow 1-2 blocks per SM depending on register usage). Let's assume 1 block per SM = 68 concurrent blocks. 15,680 / 68 = 230 waves. Still a lot of waves. If 2 blocks per SM: 136 concurrent. 15,680 / 136 = 115 waves. At 10us per block (conservative), that's 1.15ms. Reasonable. But wait, 512 threads might use many registers. If each thread uses 32 registers, that's 16K registers per block. If SM has 64K registers, that's 4 blocks. But if threads = 512, max threads per SM = 2048, so 4 blocks of 512 = 2048. Register limit would be 64K / 4 = 16K per block, or 32 registers per thread. We probably need more than 32 registers per thread for all the loads and accumulations. So maybe 2 blocks of 512 threads = 1024 threads per SM. 64K / 2 = 32K registers per block = 64 registers per thread. That might be enough. The problem with 512 threads per block is the shared memory. If we use ~27KB, and L1/SMEM partition is fixed, we might leave some memory idle but that's fine. Let me try writing this kernel. One more concern: for an 8x1 tile of pixels (8 pixels arranged in a line), the blocks are small in one dimension. Maybe arrange as 2x2=4 pixels or 2x4=8 pixels. For simplicity, let's just have each block handle 4 pixels: blockIdx maps to a 2x2 tile of output pixels. Wait, actually the simplest way is: - blockIdx.x maps to pixel index = b * H_out * W_out + h * W_out + w - Each block handles 4 consecutive pixels (e.g., 4 consecutive in the flattened spatial+batch grid) - Block size = 256 threads (64 threads per pixel) But consecutive pixels might not have contiguous memory for h,w. It might be better to arrange as 2x2 tiles for better locality. I'll map: tiles_per_row = (W_out + 1) / 2; // 2 pixels wide tile_h = blockIdx.x / tiles_per_row; tile_w = blockIdx.x % tiles_per_row; b = blockIdx.y; Pixels in block: p0 = (b, tile_h*2, tile_w*2) p1 = (b, tile_h*2, tile_w*2+1) p2 = (b, tile_h*2+1, tile_w*2) p3 = (b, tile_h*2+1, tile_w*2+1) 64 threads per pixel. Pixel p uses threads [p*64, (p+1)*64). Let me draft the kernel: ```cpp template <int KS, int STRIDE> __global__ void mbconv_kernel( const float* __restrict__ expanded, const float* __restrict__ dw_weight, const float* __restrict__ proj_weight, // [hidden_dim, C_out] const float* __restrict__ expand_bn_scale, const float* __restrict__ expand_bn_bias, const float* __restrict__ dw_bn_scale, const float* __restrict__ dw_bn_bias, const float* __restrict__ proj_bn_scale, const float* __restrict__ proj_bn_bias, const float* __restrict__ identity, float* __restrict__ out, int B, int H, int W, int H_out, int W_out, int hidden_dim, int C_out, int pad, bool has_expand, bool use_residual ) { const int PIXELS_PER_BLOCK = 4; const int THREADS_PER_PIXEL = 64; const int HIDDEN_CHUNK = 32; int tiles_w = (W_out + 1) / 2; int tile_h = blockIdx.x / tiles_w; int tile_w = blockIdx.x % tiles_w; int b = blockIdx.y; int pixel_id = threadIdx.x / THREADS_PER_PIXEL; // 0..3 int lane = threadIdx.x % THREADS_PER_PIXEL; // 0..63 // Pixel coordinates int h_out = tile_h * 2 + (pixel_id / 2); int w_out = tile_w * 2 + (pixel_id % 2); bool valid = (b < B && h_out < H_out && w_out < W_out); // Channel assignment: each thread handles 3 C_out channels (192/64 = 3) int oc0 = lane * 3; int oc1 = oc0 + 1; int oc2 = oc0 + 2; float acc0 = 0, acc1 = 0, acc2 = 0; __shared__ float smem_input[PIXELS_PER_BLOCK][HIDDEN_CHUNK][KS][KS]; __shared__ float smem_dw_wt[HIDDEN_CHUNK][KS][KS]; __shared__ float smem_dw_result[PIXELS_PER_BLOCK][HIDDEN_CHUNK]; __shared__ float smem_proj_wt[HIDDEN_CHUNK][192]; // C_out for (int h_base = 0; h_base < hidden_dim; h_base += HIDDEN_CHUNK) { int valid_h = min(HIDDEN_CHUNK, hidden_dim - h_base); // Load input int total_input = PIXELS_PER_BLOCK * valid_h * KS * KS; for (int idx = threadIdx.x; idx < total_input; idx += blockDim.x) { int p = idx / (valid_h * KS * KS); int rem = idx % (valid_h * KS * KS); int h = rem / (KS * KS); int pos = rem % (KS * KS); int kh = pos / KS; int kw = pos % KS; int h_in = (tile_h * 2 + (p / 2)) * STRIDE + kh - pad; int w_in = (tile_w * 2 + (p % 2)) * STRIDE + kw - pad; int h_global = h_base + h; if (h_global < hidden_dim) { float val = 0.0f; if (h_in >= 0 && h_in < H && w_in >= 0 && w_in < W && valid) { val = expanded[((b * hidden_dim + h_global) * H + h_in) * W + w_in]; if (has_expand) { val = val * expand_bn_scale[h_global] + expand_bn_bias[h_global]; val = fminf(fmaxf(val, 0.0f), 6.0f); } } smem_input[p][h][kh][kw] = val; } } // Load dw weights int total_dw = valid_h * KS * KS; for (int idx = threadIdx.x; idx < total_dw; idx += blockDim.x) { int h = idx / (KS * KS); int pos = idx % (KS * KS); int h_global = h_base + h; smem_dw_wt[h][pos / KS][pos % KS] = dw_weight[h_global * KS * KS + pos]; } __syncthreads(); // Compute dw_result for this pixel if (valid && lane < valid_h) { #pragma unroll for (int h = lane; h < valid_h; h += THREADS_PER_PIXEL) { // wait, lane < 64, valid_h <= 32 // Only threads with lane < valid_h participate } } // Better: use all 64 threads collaboratively for the 32 channels // Each thread handles valid_h / 64 channels, but valid_h <= 32, so many threads idle. // Let's just have threads 0..31 in each pixel compute 1 channel each. // Since pixel_id * 64 + lane, threads pixel_id*64 to pixel_id*64+31 handle dw. int dw_thread = lane; if (dw_thread < valid_h && valid) { float dw_val = 0.0f; #pragma unroll for (int kh = 0; kh < KS; ++kh) for (int kw = 0; kw < KS; ++kw) dw_val += smem_input[pixel_id][dw_thread][kh][kw] * smem_dw_wt[dw_thread][kh][kw]; int h_global = h_base + dw_thread; dw_val = dw_val * dw_bn_scale[h_global] + dw_bn_bias[h_global]; dw_val = fminf(fmaxf(dw_val, 0.0f), 6.0f); smem_dw_result[pixel_id][dw_thread] = dw_val; } // Threads 32..63 just wait __syncthreads(); // Load proj weights [valid_h][C_out] int total_proj = valid_h * C_out; for (int idx = threadIdx.x; idx < total_proj; idx += blockDim.x) { int h = idx / C_out; int c = idx % C_out; int h_global = h_base + h; smem_proj_wt[h][c] = proj_weight[h_global * C_out + c]; } __syncthreads(); // Accumulate projection for our 3 assigned channels if (valid && oc2 < C_out) { #pragma unroll for (int h = 0; h < valid_h; ++h) { float dw_val = smem_dw_result[pixel_id][h]; acc0 += dw_val * smem_proj_wt[h][oc0]; acc1 += dw_val * smem_proj_wt[h][oc1]; acc2 += dw_val * smem_proj_wt[h][oc2]; } } else if (valid && oc1 < C_out) { #pragma unroll for (int h = 0; h < valid_h; ++h) { float dw_val = smem_dw_result[pixel_id][h]; acc0 += dw_val * smem_proj_wt[h][oc0]; acc1 += dw_val * smem_proj_wt[h][oc1]; } } else if (valid && oc0 < C_out) { #pragma unroll for (int h = 0; h < valid_h; ++h) { float dw_val = smem_dw_result[pixel_id][h]; acc0 += dw_val * smem_proj_wt[h][oc0]; } } __syncthreads(); } // Write output if (valid) { if (oc0 < C_out) { float val = acc0 * proj_bn_scale[oc0] + proj_bn_bias[oc0]; if (use_residual) val += identity[((b * C_out + oc0) * H_out + h_out) * W_out + w_out]; out[((b * C_out + oc0) * H_out + h_out) * W_out + w_out] = val; } if (oc1 < C_out) { float val = acc1 * proj_bn_scale[oc1] + proj_bn_bias[oc1]; if (use_residual) val += identity[((b * C_out + oc1) * H_out + h_out) * W_out + w_out]; out[((b * C_out + oc1) * H_out + h_out) * W_out + w_out] = val; } if (oc2 < C_out) { float val = acc2 * proj_bn_scale[oc2] + proj_bn_bias[oc2]; if (use_residual) val += identity[((b * C_out + oc2) * H_out + h_out) * W_out + w_out]; out[((b * C_out + oc2) * H_out + h_out) * W_out + w_out] = val; } } } ``` Wait, there's a problem with the dw computation. For valid_h <= 32, threads 0..31 in each pixel group compute 1 channel each. But lane < valid_h means only the first valid_h threads participate. The remaining threads are idle. Also, `smem_input[PIXELS_PER_BLOCK][HIDDEN_CHUNK][KS][KS]` with KS=5 is: 4 * 32 * 5 * 5 = 3200 floats. But we're loading `PIXELS_PER_BLOCK * valid_h * KS * KS` where valid_h <= 32. If valid_h is sometimes less than 32 (e.g., in the last chunk), we only load that many. But the smem is declared with HIDDEN_CHUNK=32. That's fine. Similarly `smem_dw_result[4][32]`. One more issue: `smem_proj_wt[HIDDEN_CHUNK][192] = 32 * 192 = 6144 floats`. Total shared memory: smem_input: 3200 smem_dw_wt: 800 smem_dw_result: 128 smem_proj_wt: 6144 Total: 10,272 floats = 41KB. Actually, `smem_input[4][32][5][5]` at 4 bytes each = 4 * 32 * 25 * 4 = 12,800 bytes. `smem_dw_wt[32][5][5]` = 32 * 25 * 4 = 3,200 bytes. `smem_dw_result[4][32]` = 128 * 4 = 512 bytes. `smem_proj_wt[32][192]` = 32 * 192 * 4 = 24,576 bytes. Total = 41,088 bytes. Under 48KB. Good! But wait, with 32 banks and `smem_proj_wt[h][c]`, the addressing is `h * 192 + c`. Bank = (h * 192 + c) % 32 = c % 32. When the warp accesses `smem_proj_wt[h][oc0]`, `smem_proj_wt[h][oc1]`, `smem_proj_wt[h][oc2]`, for different threads with different oc0, there's a potential bank conflict. Oc0 = lane * 3. For lane in 0..31, oc0 in {0, 3, 6, 9, ..., 93}. Bank of oc0 = oc0 % 32. For lane=0: oc0=0, bank 0 lane=1: oc0=3, bank 3 lane=2: oc0=6, bank 6 ... lane=10: oc0=30, bank 30 lane=11: oc0=33, bank 1 lane=16: oc0=48, bank 16 lane=21: oc0=63, bank 31 lane=22: oc0=66, bank 2 ... At a single instruction, we access `smem_proj_wt[h][oc0]` for all threads in the warp. Banks accessed: oc0 % 32 for lane=0..31. Since lane*3 mod 32 generates all 32 unique values (because gcd(3,32)=1), there are no bank conflicts. Similarly, when we access `smem_proj_wt[h][oc0+1]`, the banks are (lane*3+1) % 32, also all unique. And `smem_proj_wt[h][oc0+2]`: (lane*3+2) % 32, also all unique. So no bank conflicts for the proj weight access! Great. But within a pixel group (64 threads), there are 2 warps. Warp 0 accesses lanes 0..31, warp 1 accesses lanes 32..63. For warp 1: oc0 = (32 + lane) * 3 = 96 + lane*3. oc0 % 32 = (96 % 32) + (lane*3 % 32) = lane*3 % 32. Same pattern as warp 0! If both warps try to access shared memory simultaneously, they access the same banks but from different addresses (offset by 96 floats, which is 24 banks away? Actually 96 % 32 = 0, so same banks!). This means the two warps will access the same banks, which can cause serialization. The shared memory can serve one request per bank per cycle. If two warps access the same bank in the same cycle, they serialize. However, warps are independent and might not execute the exact same instruction simultaneously. It depends on the scheduler. And even if they do, the serialization is only a 2-way conflict, not 32-way. This is probably acceptable. To mitigate, we could interleave the channel assignment so that warp 0 and warp 1 access different banks. For example: - Warp 0 threads: compute channels 0, 1, 2 and 3, 4, 5 ... wait, 64 threads * 3 channels = 192 channels. - Thread j: compute channels j*3, j*3+1, j*3+2. - Warp 0: j=0..31, channels 0..95. - Warp 1: j=32..63, channels 96..191. - For warp 1 accessing channel c=96: bank = 96 % 32 = 0. Same as warp 0 accessing c=0. - For warp 1 accessing c=97: bank = 1. Same as warp 0 accessing c=1. They always have the same bank pattern. This is unavoidable with contiguous assignment and 192 channels (multiple of 32). What if we assign channels as: - Thread j: compute channels j, j+64, j+128. Then each thread accesses channels spaced by 64. Bank = j % 32 for the first access, (j+64)%32 = j%32 for the second, (j+128)%32 = j%32 for the third. All three accesses by the same thread go to the SAME bank! That causes conflicts within a warp (since thread j and thread j+32 both access the same bank). So our current assignment is actually quite good. We'll stick with it. Alright, I'll write the complete kernel with this design. Actually, I want to rethink whether the per-block 4-pixel approach will actually be faster than PyTorch. If PyTorch reference takes 1-2ms for the whole MBConv, and my kernel takes say 2-3ms, then I've lost! But maybe PyTorch is slower than I think for depthwise conv with such extreme group count. Actually, I just recalled that depthwise conv in cuDNN is often not very optimized for large group counts. In some cases, PyTorch falls back to a slow implementation. Let me search my memory: for depthwise conv with groups == in_channels, PyTorch uses the `miopenDepthwiseConvolution` or cuDNN's grouped conv path. For a 5x5 kernel with stride 2 and 672 groups, it might launch 6720 separate convolutions (batch * groups) or use a specialized algorithm. In fact, I've seen cases where depthwise conv with large channels is surprisingly slow because each group operates on a single channel, and the arithmetic intensity is very low (only k*k FLOPs per k*k pixels of input loaded). For a 5x5 depthwise conv, you load 25 floats and do 25 FMAs. AI = 25 FMAs / (25 * 4 bytes) = 0.25 FMA/byte. That's heavily bandwidth bound, and the actual throughput might be limited by how efficiently the loads can be coalesced across groups. In the reference, the depthwise conv alone might take 2-3ms or more. If so, a fused kernel that eliminates the intermediate memory write and also fuses with the pointwise (which has much better AI) could win big. For the projection pointwise conv: [672, 192] for each pixel. This is a GEMV/GEMM that cuDNN handles via implicit GEMM. For 112x112=12544 pixels per image and batch=10, there are 125440 independent GEMVs. cuDNN might batch these into a GEMM of [125440, 672] x [672, 192], which is a decent size (125440x672x192). That actually runs very fast on tensor cores! Wait, this is important. PyTorch's Conv2d for 1x1 conv with large batch*spatial is basically a batched GEMM, which is highly optimized. The pointwise conv alone might take only 0.2-0.3ms. So the total reference might be: - Expand 1x1: 0.3ms (cuDNN GEMM, batched) - Depthwise 5x5: 2.0ms (inefficient due to low AI) - Pointwise 1x1: 0.2ms - BN/ReLU/add: negligible Total: ~2.5ms My fused kernel can eliminate: - The expanded activation write/read (~0.25ms) - The dw output write/read (~0.125ms) - Kernel launch overhead - And hopefully the dw execution is faster than cuDNN's generic path. So if my kernel takes 1.5ms vs 2.5ms reference, that's a 1.67x speedup. This seems plausible. Let me proceed with writing the kernel. I'll write the kernel to be clean and correct. Here's the plan: 1. ModelNew init: same constructors, extract weights from PyTorch layers 2. Forward: - If training: use PyTorch ops - If eval: a. Compute expand 1x1 conv if needed b. Precompute fused BN scales/biases c. Transpose projection weights to [hidden_dim, C_out] d. Call CUDA kernel Wait, I also need to think about whether the model parameters are on CPU or GPU. The test code likely moves the model to the same device as the input. I should handle `.to(x.device)` logic or just assume weights are on the correct device. Actually, nn.Module weights are automatically tensors, and when the module is moved to GPU, the weights move too. So in forward(), the weights will be on the correct device. One caveat: the CUDA kernel will be compiled at module load time. The weights might be on CPU during initialization but on GPU during forward(). The kernel receives device pointers, so that's fine. Let me now write the code. I'll create two versions: a specialized one for the test parameters and a generic fallback.

But doing one GEMV per spatial position would still be very inefficient (small sizes, repeated memory reads of weight matrix). Actually the main bottleneck is definitely the depthwise convolution. The problem with depthwise convolution is that it has poor compute density because each output channel reads a different set of input channels while the weights are small. A key optimization for depthwise convolution on H100 is to use Tensor Cores, though they're not very efficient for this. Or we can use a more efficient memory layout. reasoning
Let me try a different approach. Instead of using the cpp_extension with the specific library issues, let me check if there's a way to avoid the CXXABI issue. One common workaround is to set `LD_LIBRARY_PATH` before importing anything, or to use `torch.classes` differently. Another approach is to use `extra_ldflags` to specify library paths. But actually, looking at the error more carefully - it seems the issue is that the compiled .so file requires a newer libstdc++. The importlib loads it, and it fails because `/lib/x86_64-linux-gnu/libstdc++.so.6` is too old. But `/opt/conda/lib/libstdc++.so.6` should be newer. Let me try using `extra_ldflags=['-Wl,-rpath,/opt/conda/lib']` to embed the rpath in the shared object. This way when the .so is loaded, it will look in /opt/conda/lib first.
